<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Raphidocelis subcapitata (= Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata ) provides an insight into genome evolution and environmental adaptations in the Sphaeropleales"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2018 8:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-05-23"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The Sphaeropleales are a dominant group of green algae, which contain species important to freshwater ecosystems and those that have potential applied usages. In particular, Raphidocelis subcapitata is widely used worldwide for bioassays in toxicological risk assessments. However, there are few comparative genome analyses of the Sphaeropleales. To reveal genome evolution in the Sphaeropleales based on well-resolved phylogenetic relationships, nuclear, mitochondrial, and plastid genomes were sequenced in this study. The plastid genome provides insights into the phylogenetic relationships of R. subcapitata, which is located in the most basal lineage of the four species in the family Selenastraceae. The mitochondrial genome shows dynamic evolutionary histories with intron expansion in the Selenastraceae. The 51.2 Mbp nuclear genome of R. subcapitata, encoding 13,383 protein-coding genes, is more compact than the genome of its closely related oil-rich species, Monoraphidium neglectum (Selenastraceae), Tetradesmus obliquus (Scenedesmaceae), and Chromochloris zofingiensis (Chromochloridaceae); however, the four species share most of their genes. The Sphaeropleales possess a large number of genes for glycerolipid metabolism and sugar assimilation, which suggests that this order is capable of both heterotrophic and mixotrophic lifestyles in nature. Comparison of transporter genes suggests that the Sphaeropleales can adapt to different natural environmental conditions, such as salinity and low metal concentrations."/>

    <meta name="description" content="The Sphaeropleales are a dominant group of green algae, which contain species important to freshwater ecosystems and those that have potential applied usages. In particular, Raphidocelis subcapitata is widely used worldwide for bioassays in toxicological risk assessments. However, there are few comparative genome analyses of the Sphaeropleales. To reveal genome evolution in the Sphaeropleales based on well-resolved phylogenetic relationships, nuclear, mitochondrial, and plastid genomes were sequenced in this study. The plastid genome provides insights into the phylogenetic relationships of R. subcapitata, which is located in the most basal lineage of the four species in the family Selenastraceae. The mitochondrial genome shows dynamic evolutionary histories with intron expansion in the Selenastraceae. The 51.2 Mbp nuclear genome of R. subcapitata, encoding 13,383 protein-coding genes, is more compact than the genome of its closely related oil-rich species, Monoraphidium neglectum (Selenastraceae), Tetradesmus obliquus (Scenedesmaceae), and Chromochloris zofingiensis (Chromochloridaceae); however, the four species share most of their genes. The Sphaeropleales possess a large number of genes for glycerolipid metabolism and sugar assimilation, which suggests that this order is capable of both heterotrophic and mixotrophic lifestyles in nature. Comparison of transporter genes suggests that the Sphaeropleales can adapt to different natural environmental conditions, such as salinity and low metal concentrations."/>

                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The Sphaeropleales are a dominant group of green algae, which contain species important to freshwater ecosystems and those that have potential applied usages. In particular, <i>Raphidocelis subcapitata</i> is widely used worldwide for bioassays in toxicological risk assessments. However, there are few comparative genome analyses of the Sphaeropleales. To reveal genome evolution in the Sphaeropleales based on well-resolved phylogenetic relationships, nuclear, mitochondrial, and plastid genomes were sequenced in this study. The plastid genome provides insights into the phylogenetic relationships of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, which is located in the most basal lineage of the four species in the family Selenastraceae. The mitochondrial genome shows dynamic evolutionary histories with intron expansion in the Selenastraceae. The 51.2 Mbp nuclear genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, encoding 13,383 protein-coding genes, is more compact than the genome of its closely related oil-rich species, <i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i> (Selenastraceae), <i>Tetradesmus obliquus</i> (Scenedesmaceae), and <i>Chromochloris zofingiensis</i> (Chromochloridaceae); however, the four species share most of their genes. The Sphaeropleales possess a large number of genes for glycerolipid metabolism and sugar assimilation, which suggests that this order is capable of both heterotrophic and mixotrophic lifestyles in nature. Comparison of transporter genes suggests that the Sphaeropleales can adapt to different natural environmental conditions, such as salinity and low metal concentrations.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Chlorophyceae are genetically, morphologically, and ecologically diverse class of green algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Phylogeny and molecular evolution of the green algae. CRC. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 1â46 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d61147e343">1</a></sup>. The group is dominant, particularly in freshwater, and plays important roles in global ecosystems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Falkowski, P. G. et al. The evolution of modern eukaryotic phytoplankton. Science 305, 354â60 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d61147e347">2</a></sup>. The Chlorophyceae are composed of five taxonomic orders: Sphaeropleales, Chlamydomonadales, Chaetophorales, Chaetopeltidales, and Oedogoniales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Phylogeny and molecular evolution of the green algae. CRC. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 1â46 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d61147e351">1</a></sup>. The Sphaeropleales are a large group, and contain some of the most common freshwater species (e.g. <i>Scenedesmus</i>, <i>Desmodesmus</i>, <i>Tetradesmus</i>, and <i>Raphidocelis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wolf, M., Buchheim, M., Hegewald, E., Krienitz, L. &amp; Hepperle, D. Phylogenetic position of the Sphaeropleaceae (Chlorophyta). Plant Syst. Evol. 230, 161â171 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d61147e368">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Krienitz, L., Bock, C., Nozaki, H. &amp; Wolf, M. SSU rRNA gene phylogeny of morphospecies affiliated to the bioassay alga âSelenastrum capricornutumâ recovered the polyphyletic origin of crescent-shaped Chlorophyta. J. Phycol. 47, 880â893 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d61147e371">4</a></sup>, including some species used in applications such as bioassays and biofuel production. In particular, <i>Raphidocelis subcapitata</i> and <i>Desmodesmus subspicatus</i> are recommended for ecotoxicological bioassays by the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) (TG201, <a href="http://www.oecd.org/">http://www.oecd.org/</a>) because they have higher growth rates and greater sensitivity to various substances than other algae.</p><p>Genome evolution in the Sphaeropleales is little understood compared to that of the Chlamydomonadales. In the Chlamydomonadales, genomes of four species (<i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i>, <i>C</i>. <i>eustigma</i>, <i>Gonium pectorale</i>, and <i>Volvox carteri</i> f. <i>nagariensis</i>) have been sequenced and analyzedÂ thus far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Merchant, S. S. et al. The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science 318, 245â50 (2007)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d61147e410">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hanschen, E. R. et al. The Gonium pectorale genome demonstrates co-option of cell cycle regulation during the evolution of multicellularity. Nat. Commun. 7, 11370 (2016)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d61147e410_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223â226 (2010)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d61147e410_2">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Hirooka, S. et al. Acidophilic green algal genome provides insights into adaptation to an acidic environment. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 114, E8304âE8313 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d61147e413">8</a></sup>. Comparative genome analyses have provided great insights into the evolution of green algae traits, such as flagella<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Merchant, S. S. et al. The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science 318, 245â50 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d61147e417">5</a></sup>, multicellularity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hanschen, E. R. et al. The Gonium pectorale genome demonstrates co-option of cell cycle regulation during the evolution of multicellularity. Nat. Commun. 7, 11370 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d61147e421">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223â226 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d61147e424">7</a></sup>, and sexual reproduction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Ferris, P. et al. Evolution of an expanded sex-determining locus in Volvox. Science 328, 351â354 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d61147e428">9</a></sup>. In contrast, genome analyses of the Sphaeropleales are rare; only three genomes, that of <i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e435">10</a></sup>, <i>Tetradesmus obliquus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Carreres, B. M. et al. Draft genome sequence of the oleaginous green alga Tetradesmus obliquus UTEX 393. Genome Announc. 5, e01449â16 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d61147e441">11</a></sup>, and <i>Chromochloris zofingiensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Roth, M. S. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly and transcriptome of the green alga Chromochloris zofingiensis illuminates astaxanthin production. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 114, E4296âE4305 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d61147e447">12</a></sup>, have been sequenced. Furthermore, their comparative analyses have not been performed. The comparative analyses should provide insights into genome evolution of the Sphaeropleales and adaptation to different freshwater environments. <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> contains large amounts of lipids under a wide range of pH and salt conditions, and thus shows potential for lipid production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Bogen, C. et al. Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour. Technol. 133, 622â626 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d61147e458">13</a></sup>. Its genome is 68 Mbp and encodes 16,761 proteins, including many genes related to carbohydrate metabolism and fatty acid biosynthesis, and indicates that the vegetative cells have diploid characters. <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, which was formerly classified as <i>Acutodesmus obliquus</i> or <i>Scenedesmus obliquus</i>, is a model organism in the Sphaeropleales for biofuel production and organellar genetics. Its nuclear genome is 109 Mbp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Carreres, B. M. et al. Draft genome sequence of the oleaginous green alga Tetradesmus obliquus UTEX 393. Genome Announc. 5, e01449â16 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d61147e474">11</a></sup>, but its detailed genome structure (i.e. gene models and annotations) has not been described. Recently, the nuclear genome of <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> (=<i>Chlorella zofingiensis</i>) has been sequenced; the genome size is 58 Mbp and it encodes 15,274 proteins. In the case of organellar genomes, the mitochondrial genome has unusual codon usages (i.e. UAG for leucine, not as a stop codon, and UCA as a stop codon, not for serine)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hayashi-Ishimaru, Y., Ohama, T., Kawatsu, Y., Nakamura, K. &amp; Osawa, S. UAG is a sense codon in several chlorophycean mitochondria. Curr. Genet. 30, 29â33 (1996)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d61147e488">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Nedelcu, A. M., Lee, R. W., Lemieux, C., Gray, M. W. &amp; Burger, G. The complete mitochondrial DNA sequence of Scenedesmus obliquus reflects an intermediate stage in the evolution of the green algal mitochondrial genome. Genome Res. 10, 819â831 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d61147e491">15</a></sup>, and a split <i>cox2</i> (<i>cox2a</i>, and <i>cox2b</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="PÃ©rez-MartÃ­nez, X. et al. Subunit II of cytochrome c oxidase in chlamydomonad algae is a heterodimer encoded by two independent nuclear genes. J. Biol. Chem. 276, 11302â11309 (2001)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d61147e505">16</a></sup>. The <i>cox2b</i> gene is located in the nuclear genome, and thus it appears to have been transferred to the nuclear genome via an endosymbiotic gene transfer (EGT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="RodrÃ­guez-Salinas, E. et al. Lineage-specific fragmentation and nuclear relocation of the mitochondrial cox2 gene in chlorophycean green algae (Chlorophyta). Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 64, 166â176 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d61147e512">17</a></sup>. In the Chlamydomonadales, both <i>cox2a</i> and <i>cox2b</i> are in the nuclear genome; therefore, the genes of <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> are thought to be an intermediate character in EGT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Funes, S. et al. A green algal apicoplast ancestor. Science 298, 2155 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d61147e529">18</a></sup>. These mitochondrial characters are conserved in the Sphaeropleales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="RodrÃ­guez-Salinas, E. et al. Lineage-specific fragmentation and nuclear relocation of the mitochondrial cox2 gene in chlorophycean green algae (Chlorophyta). Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 64, 166â176 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d61147e533">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡, K., Lewis, P. O., GonzÃ¡lez-Halphen, D. &amp; Lewis, L. A. Gene arrangement convergence, diverse intron content, and genetic code modifications in mitochondrial genomes of Sphaeropleales (Chlorophyta). Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 2170â80 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d61147e536">19</a></sup>. The plastid genomes of the Sphaeropleales have fewer structural variations than the Chlamydomonadales and the OCC clade (Oedogoniales, Chaetopeltidales, and Chaetophorales)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡, K., Lewis, P. O. &amp; Lewis, L. A. Chloroplast phylogenomic data from the green algal order Sphaeropleales (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta) reveal complex patterns of sequence evolution. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 98, 176â183 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d61147e540">20</a></sup>. Therefore, the group is interesting for studying organellar and nuclear genome evolution in green algae.</p><p><i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> NIES-35 was formerly known as <i>Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata</i> or â<i>Selenastrum capricornutum</i>â<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Krienitz, L., Bock, C., Nozaki, H. &amp; Wolf, M. SSU rRNA gene phylogeny of morphospecies affiliated to the bioassay alga âSelenastrum capricornutumâ recovered the polyphyletic origin of crescent-shaped Chlorophyta. J. Phycol. 47, 880â893 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d61147e558">4</a></sup>. On the basis of 18S rRNA phylogeny, <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> belongs to the family Selenastraceae, order Sphaeropleales, but its phylogenetic position in the group has not been resolved<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Krienitz, L., Bock, C., Nozaki, H. &amp; Wolf, M. SSU rRNA gene phylogeny of morphospecies affiliated to the bioassay alga âSelenastrum capricornutumâ recovered the polyphyletic origin of crescent-shaped Chlorophyta. J. Phycol. 47, 880â893 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d61147e569">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fawley, M. W., Dean, M. L., Dimmer, S. K. &amp; Fawley, K. P. Evaluating the morphospecies concept in the Selenastraceae (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta). J. Phycol. 42, 142â154 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d61147e572">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Lemieux, C., Vincent, A. T., Labarre, A., Otis, C. &amp; Turmel, M. Chloroplast phylogenomic analysis of chlorophyte green algae identifies a novel lineage sister to the Sphaeropleales (Chlorophyceae). BMC Evol. Biol. 15, 264 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d61147e575">22</a></sup>. Although well-resolved phylogenetic relationships have been recently reported for the Sphaeropleales, using plastid or mitochondrial genome-encoded proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡, K., Lewis, P. O., GonzÃ¡lez-Halphen, D. &amp; Lewis, L. A. Gene arrangement convergence, diverse intron content, and genetic code modifications in mitochondrial genomes of Sphaeropleales (Chlorophyta). Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 2170â80 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d61147e579">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡, K., Lewis, P. O. &amp; Lewis, L. A. Chloroplast phylogenomic data from the green algal order Sphaeropleales (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta) reveal complex patterns of sequence evolution. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 98, 176â183 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d61147e582">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Farwagi, A. A., FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡, K. &amp; McManus, H. A. Phylogenetic patterns of gene rearrangements in four mitochondrial genomes from the green algal family Hydrodictyaceae (Sphaeropleales, Chlorophyceae). BMC Genomics 16, 826 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d61147e585">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Lee, H.-G. et al. Unique mitochondrial genome structure of the green algal strain YC001 (Sphaeropleales, Chlorophyta), with morphological observations. Phycologia 55, 72â78 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d61147e588">24</a></sup>, the organellar genomes of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> have not been sequenced. In this study, we sequenced the nuclear, mitochondrial, and plastid genomes of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, and compared them to other Sphaeropleales species genomes to reveal genome evolution in the order based on well-resolved phylogenetic relationships and to understand their genetic background in relation to high sensitivity to chemicals (e.g. metals). The plastid and mitochondrial genomes provide insights into the phylogenetic relationships of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and complex evolutionary histories in the order Sphaeropleales. The nuclear genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> is the most compact in this order, and comparison of proteins indicates that the Sphaeropleales can adapt to a variety of nutrient and environmental conditions.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Phylogenetic analyses</h3><p>To infer the phylogenetic position of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> within the Sphaeropleales, we performed phylogenetic analyses using two datasets: 55 plastid-encoded or 13 mitochondrion-encoded proteins (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig1">1</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S1</a>). In both trees, <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> formed a monophyletic group with the other Selenastraceae species, <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, <i>Ourococcus multisporus</i>, and <i>Kirchneriella aperta</i>. There was robust support (BPâ=â100 and BPPâ=â1.00) for inclusion of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> in the Sphaeropleales; however, the topologies of the trees were different. The tree using plastid-encoded proteins resolves the phylogenetic relationships better than the mitochondrial tree because it had higher supporting values (BPs at all nodes â¥70) and was based on more amino acids than that based on mitochondrion-encoded proteins. In the plastid-based tree, <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was the most basal lineage in the Selenastraceae (BPâ=â94 and BPPâ=â1.00) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig1">1</a>). <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> and <i>O</i>. <i>multisporus</i> were sister species showing robust support (BPâ=â99 and BPPâ=â1.00). The Selenastraceae was a sister group to <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, <i>Neochloris aquatica</i>, and <i>Chlorotetraedron incus</i> (BPâ=â99, BPPâ=â1.00). <i>Chromochloris zofingiensis</i> was monophyletic with the Selenastraceae, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, <i>N</i>. <i>aquatica</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>incus</i>, with moderate support (BPâ=â71, BPPâ=â0.9993). Therefore, <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> was probably the most basal among the four species with available nuclear genomes of the Sphaeropleales.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="865" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>ML phylogenetic tree of the Sphaeropleales using 55 plastid-encoded proteins. The best tree was reconstructed using a concatenated dataset of 11,649 amino acids. Values at the nodes represent bootstrap supports (BP) of 200 replicates (right) and Bayesian posterior probabilities (BPP) (left). BPâ&lt;â50 or BPPâ&lt;â0.9 are not shown. Bold lines represent BPâ=â100 and BPPâ=â1.00.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Evolution of plastid and mitochondrial genomes</h3><p>In the Sphaeropleales, 17 mitochondrial and 11 plastid genomes have been submitted so far to comparative analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e755">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Nedelcu, A. M., Lee, R. W., Lemieux, C., Gray, M. W. &amp; Burger, G. The complete mitochondrial DNA sequence of Scenedesmus obliquus reflects an intermediate stage in the evolution of the green algal mitochondrial genome. Genome Res. 10, 819â831 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d61147e758">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡, K., Lewis, P. O., GonzÃ¡lez-Halphen, D. &amp; Lewis, L. A. Gene arrangement convergence, diverse intron content, and genetic code modifications in mitochondrial genomes of Sphaeropleales (Chlorophyta). Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 2170â80 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d61147e761">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡, K., Lewis, P. O. &amp; Lewis, L. A. Chloroplast phylogenomic data from the green algal order Sphaeropleales (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta) reveal complex patterns of sequence evolution. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 98, 176â183 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d61147e764">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Farwagi, A. A., FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡, K. &amp; McManus, H. A. Phylogenetic patterns of gene rearrangements in four mitochondrial genomes from the green algal family Hydrodictyaceae (Sphaeropleales, Chlorophyceae). BMC Genomics 16, 826 (2015)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d61147e767">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lee, H.-G. et al. Unique mitochondrial genome structure of the green algal strain YC001 (Sphaeropleales, Chlorophyta), with morphological observations. Phycologia 55, 72â78 (2016)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d61147e767_1">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="KÃ¼ck, U., Jekosch, K. &amp; Holzamer, P. DNA sequence analysis of the complete mitochondrial genome of the green alga Scenedesmus obliquus: evidence for UAG being a leucine and UCA being a non-sense codon. Gene 253, 13â18 (2000)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d61147e767_2">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="de Cambiaire, J.-C., Otis, C., Lemieux, C. &amp; Turmel, M. The complete chloroplast genome sequence of the chlorophycean green alga Scenedesmus obliquus reveals a compact gene organization and a biased distribution of genes on the two DNA strands. BMC Evol. Biol. 6, 37 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d61147e770">26</a></sup>. We sequenced the complete mitochondrial and plastid genomes of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and compared these sequences to those of other Sphaeropleales to reveal organellar genome evolution of the order, and particularly of the family Selenastraceae. The mitochondrial genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was circular and 44,268âbp in size (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S2a</a>); this genome contained a large tandem repeat region with a 10-mer unit repeated at least 11 times at position 21,665. Protein-coding genes could be translated following the genetic code of the mitochondria of <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hayashi-Ishimaru, Y., Ohama, T., Kawatsu, Y., Nakamura, K. &amp; Osawa, S. UAG is a sense codon in several chlorophycean mitochondria. Curr. Genet. 30, 29â33 (1996)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d61147e795">14</a></sup>. There were 13 conserved protein-coding genes (3 cytochrome oxygenases, 1 cytochrome b, 7 NADH dehydrogenase subunits, and 2 ATP synthase subunits), 6 fragmented rRNAs, and 28 tRNAs; as observed for other Sphaeropleales species, 16S rRNA and 23S rRNA were separated into two and four fragments, respectively (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S1</a>). Cox2 was split and its N-terminus (Cox2a) was encoded in the mitochondrial genome, similar to other mitochondrial genomes. Sphaeropleales species encode the C-terminus of Cox2 (Cox2b) in the nuclear genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="RodrÃ­guez-Salinas, E. et al. Lineage-specific fragmentation and nuclear relocation of the mitochondrial cox2 gene in chlorophycean green algae (Chlorophyta). Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 64, 166â176 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d61147e802">17</a></sup>, and this was indeed the case in <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> (see below). <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> had 11 introns in <i>cox1</i>, <i>cob</i>, and <i>rrl4</i>, and 11 intronic open reading frames (ORFs), possibly encoding LAGLIDADG or GIY-YIG endonucleases. Except for an intron in <i>rrl4</i>, the introns of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> were inserted at the same positions as that of at least one other Sphaeropleales species (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S3aâc</a>), suggesting that they had the same origin. Selenastraceae species contained larger amounts of intronic sequences in the mitochondrial genomes than other Sphaeropleales species (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig2">2a</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S3aâc</a>), suggesting that there was a gain in introns in a common ancestor of the Selenastraceae. ProgressiveMauve analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Darling, A. E., Mau, B. &amp; Perna, N. T. progressiveMauve: multiple genome alignment with gene gain, loss and rearrangement. PLoS One 5, e11147 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d61147e848">27</a></sup> was performed to reveal the genome arrangements in the Selenastraceae. The gene order in the mitochondrial genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was identical to those of <i>K</i>. <i>aperta</i>, <i>O</i>. <i>multisporus</i>, and <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>; however, there was a divergent region in an intron of <i>cob</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S4</a>). Overall, <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, <i>K</i>. <i>aperta</i>, and <i>O</i>. <i>multisporus</i> had genome structures with high similarities, although <i>K</i>. <i>aperta</i> possessed longer introns in <i>rrl2</i> and <i>rrl4</i>. In contrast, <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> had larger intergenic regions and introns (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig2">2a</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S4</a>), which may have been caused by a secondary genome size expansion in this species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="330" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Size distributions of organellar genomes of <i>Raphidocelis subcapitata</i> and other Sphaeropleales species. (<b>a</b>) Mitochondrial genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>. (<b>b</b>) Plastid genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>. Blue, orange, and grey bars show the total sizes of coding sequences (CDSs), introns, and spacer regions, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The plastid genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was circularly mapped and 128,080âbp in size, including two 18,858âbp inverted repeats (IRs) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S2b</a>). Sixty-nine conserved protein-coding genes, 6 rRNAs, 30 tRNAs, and 7 intronic ORFs were found in the plastid genome (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S2</a>). The IR had 1 <i>psbA</i>, 3 rRNAs, and 3 tRNAs. Two introns were inserted in <i>psaA</i> and 4 introns in 2 copies of <i>rrl</i>, all of which possessed an intronic ORF encoding LAGLIDADG endonuclease. Exceptionally, there was an ORF encoding maturase in an intergenic region between <i>psbE</i> and <i>psaA-1</i>; <i>psaA</i> was split into three fragments, which were dispersed in the genome, suggesting that they could be transcribed with <i>trans-</i>splicing, as in <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="de Cambiaire, J.-C., Otis, C., Lemieux, C. &amp; Turmel, M. The complete chloroplast genome sequence of the chlorophycean green alga Scenedesmus obliquus reveals a compact gene organization and a biased distribution of genes on the two DNA strands. BMC Evol. Biol. 6, 37 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1011">26</a></sup>. Among the Sphaeropleales species studied, <i>M</i>. <i>homosphaera</i> contained the smallest plastid genome (102.7 kbp), and <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> the second smallest, mainly because of small intergenic regions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig2">2b</a>). Because the phylogenetic relationships between <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>homosphaera</i> are unclear, we cannot determine whether the genome reduction is derived. Of the three Selenastraceae species, <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and <i>K</i>. <i>aperta</i> showed a completely conserved gene set (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S2</a>), with <i>K</i>. <i>aperta</i> possessing a larger number of introns and intergenic regions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig2">2b</a>). <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> had an additional <i>atpF</i> in the IRs, one of which was fragmented; thus, it may be a pseudogene. Based on phylogenetic relationships, IRs increased in length in <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> after branching in the Selenastraceae. The gene orders of <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> and <i>K</i>. <i>aperta</i> were identical, but <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> contained an inversion of <i>psbK</i>â<i>psbC</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S5</a>), suggesting that this inversion occurred independently.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">General features of the nuclear genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>
                        </h3><p>We sequenced 4.1 Gbp reads using Illumina MiSeq. Based on assembly and scaffolding, we obtained 417 scaffolds (â¥1 kbp). Major scaffolds (â¥5âkb) were 51,162,697âbp in total (300 scaffolds) (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Tab1">1</a>). The assembly size was similar to the estimated genome size (46,790,751âbp), based on a k-mer analysis (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S6</a>). To confirm the completeness of the genome, we performed BUSCO analysis, which assesses genome completeness to find the Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCOs) in a target genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1134">28</a></sup>. The analysis was based on the eukaryote dataset, and showed 91.7% complete BUSCOs, which was higher than those of <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i>, <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, and <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> (84.5%, 58.5%, and 79.9%, respectively) (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S3</a>). Additionally, 95.8% and 95.6% of the RNA-seq reads were mapped into â¥1âkb and â¥5âkb scaffolds, respectively. Therefore, we obtained a nearly complete genome for <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>. The 300 major scaffolds were annotated using the funannotate pipeline based on <i>ab initio</i> and transcripts-based gene prediction. Repeat sequences (12.45% of the major scaffolds) were masked, and most (10.5% of the major scaffolds) were simple repeats. At least 5 rRNAs, 46 tRNAs, and 13,383 protein-coding genes were predicted. Additionally, 11,902 protein-coding genes (88.9% of the prediction) were detected by RNA-seq. The remaining 11.1% of gene models were not mapped with the RNA-seq reads; therefore, they may be rarely expressed genes and some may be pseudogenes. The protein-coding genes contained 76,178 introns at a density of 5.7 introns per gene.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistical comparison of nuclear genomes of <i>R. subcapitata, M. neglectum, T. obliquus, C. zofingiensis</i> and <i>C. reinhardtii</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The nuclear genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was smallest in the Sphaeropleales (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Tab1">1</a>). Thus far, the nuclear genomes of <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> have been sequenced<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1751">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Carreres, B. M. et al. Draft genome sequence of the oleaginous green alga Tetradesmus obliquus UTEX 393. Genome Announc. 5, e01449â16 (2017)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1751_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Roth, M. S. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly and transcriptome of the green alga Chromochloris zofingiensis illuminates astaxanthin production. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 114, E4296âE4305 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1754">12</a></sup>. The genomes differed greatly from that of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> in size, gene number, coding capacity, and GC-content. <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> has a 107.7 Mbp genome, which is nearly twice as large as that of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, but the two species possess a similar number of genes. The coding percentages of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> were 44.0% and 15.7%, respectively. These results suggest that the larger genome of <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> is caused by a large amount of non-coding regions (i.e. intergenic regions and introns). The genomes of <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> (68 Mbp) and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> (58 Mbp) were also smaller but with higher coding percentages (25.7% and 39%<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Roth, M. S. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly and transcriptome of the green alga Chromochloris zofingiensis illuminates astaxanthin production. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 114, E4296âE4305 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1809">12</a></sup>, respectively) than that of <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>. However, <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> possessed 16,761, and 15,274 genes, respectively, at least ~2,000 genes more than <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>. We compared gene families, which were defined by TreeFam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ruan, J. et al. TreeFam: 2008 update. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D735â740 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1845">29</a></sup>, among <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i>. Most of the families (58.2â69.8% of the total) were shared by them (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig3">3a</a>). <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> had 4,063 gene families, ~300 more gene families than the others (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig3">3b</a>). However, only 296 unique gene families were found in <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i>, which was similar to that of the others (160â218 gene families) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig3">3a</a>). Functional analyses using KEGG categories<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kanehisa, M., Goto, S., Sato, Y., Furumichi, M. &amp; Tanabe, M. KEGG for integration and interpretation of large-scale molecular data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D109â114 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1897">30</a></sup> showed that most of the functions were shared in the Sphaeropleales (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig3">3c</a>). <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> possessed the largest number of genes among the Sphaeropleales families considered in the study (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig3">3b</a>). Therefore, gene expansion in <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> probably originated in the past as a complete or partial duplication of the genome. Bogen <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1922">10</a></sup> reported that <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> had a diploid character because their investigation of a âcontig length vs. read countâ plot showed homozygous and heterozygous contigs. In this plot, contigs of homozygous loci have more than twice the read coverage than those of heterozygous loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wibberg, D. et al. Establishment and interpretation of the genome sequence of the phytopathogenic fungus Rhizoctonia solani AG1-IB isolate 7/3/14. J. Biotechnol. 167, 142â155 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1932">31</a></sup>. However, such characters were not found in <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S7a,b</a>), and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Roth, M. S. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly and transcriptome of the green alga Chromochloris zofingiensis illuminates astaxanthin production. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 114, E4296âE4305 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1958">12</a></sup>. Therefore, we consider that vegetative cells of the Sphaeropleales are basically haploid. The haploid character, and the small size and simplicity of the <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> genome are of great advantage for transformation and genome editing. Interestingly, GC-content of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was 71.6%, which was higher than that of <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Tab1">1</a>). Roth <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Roth, M. S. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly and transcriptome of the green alga Chromochloris zofingiensis illuminates astaxanthin production. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 114, E4296âE4305 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d61147e1999">12</a></sup> indicated that the high GC-content of several algae was associated with more fragmentary assemblies. However, the <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> genome had higher GC-content than <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> and <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> despite the less fragmentary assembly of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S3</a>). Therefore, the genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> might have high GC-content in nature. Codon usage of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was highly biased; we found that although all codons were used, there was a greater proportion of GC at synonymous codons, e.g. cysteine was coded for by 20% UGU and 80% UGC codons (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S4</a>). Additionally, the GC-content of transcripts (coding regions) in <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was 74.6%, which was higher than that of spacer regions (66.2%). These results suggest that the GC bias is related to the high gene density and coding length of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>. In mammalian species, it is known that high GC-content is positively correlated to gene density<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Sumner, A. T., de la Torre, J. &amp; Stuppia, L. The distribution of genes on chromosomes: A cytological approach. J. Mol. Evol. 37, 117â122 (1993)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2063">32</a></sup> and coding length<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Pozzoli, U. et al. Both selective and neutral processes drive GC content evolution in the human genome. BMC Evol. Biol. 8, 99 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2067">33</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="696" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Comparison of gene families among <i>Raphidocelis subcapitata, Monoraphidium neglectum, Tetradesmus obliquus</i>, and <i>Chromochloris zofingiensis</i>. (<b>a</b>) Venn diagram of the gene families of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, and <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>. (<b>b</b>) The number and size of gene families. Gene families consisting of multiple genes are shown in red, grey, and orange according to their family size (two, three, and more than four, respectively). (<b>c</b>) Functional comparison of the genes according to KEGG classification.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Restricted endosymbiotic gene transfer of mitochondrial genes in the Sphaeropleales</h3><p>Species in the Sphaeropleales have split <i>cox2</i> (<i>cox2a</i> and <i>cox2b</i>) genes, with <i>cox2a</i> located in the mitochondrial genome and <i>cox2b</i> located in the nuclear genome, based on the difference in codon usage between the mitochondria and nucleus and the presence of spliceosomal introns. Because the Chlamydomonadales have both split <i>cox2</i> genes in their nuclear genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="PÃ©rez-MartÃ­nez, X. et al. Subunit II of cytochrome c oxidase in chlamydomonad algae is a heterodimer encoded by two independent nuclear genes. J. Biol. Chem. 276, 11302â11309 (2001)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2151">16</a></sup>, the Sphaeropleales appear to show an intermediate trait of gene migration to the nucleus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="RodrÃ­guez-Salinas, E. et al. Lineage-specific fragmentation and nuclear relocation of the mitochondrial cox2 gene in chlorophycean green algae (Chlorophyta). Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 64, 166â176 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2155">17</a></sup>.</p><p>To gain further insights into EGT from the mitochondrial genome in the Sphaeropleales, we searched for <i>cox2b</i> and nuclear mitochondrial DNA segments (NUMTs), which are nuclear sequences that are similar to the mitochondrial genome sequences. We found a <i>cox2b</i> gene in <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i>. <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> possessed two copies of <i>cox2b</i> with N terminal sequences that differed from those reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="RodrÃ­guez-Salinas, E. et al. Lineage-specific fragmentation and nuclear relocation of the mitochondrial cox2 gene in chlorophycean green algae (Chlorophyta). Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 64, 166â176 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2197">17</a></sup>. All of the <i>cox2b</i> possessed a ~50 aa N-terminal extension, which was very similar to that of the Chlamydomonadales (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S8</a>), strongly supporting the conclusion that <i>cox2b</i> migrated to the nucleus in a common ancestor of the Chlamydomonadales and Sphaeropleales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="RodrÃ­guez-Salinas, E. et al. Lineage-specific fragmentation and nuclear relocation of the mitochondrial cox2 gene in chlorophycean green algae (Chlorophyta). Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 64, 166â176 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2210">17</a></sup>.</p><p>We also performed homology searching of the mitochondrial sequences against the nuclear sequences. We did not find mitochondrial-encoded genes in nuclear protein-coding genes; however, we found some NUMTs in intergenic regions or introns of the nuclear genomes. <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> possessed 27 NUMTs with 30â119âbp with 88â100% similarity (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig4">4</a>; Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S5</a>), and <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> possessed 17 NUMTs with 34â350âbp with 83â100% similarity. <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> possessed 94 NUMTs, a greater number than in <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, with 31â279âbp with 82â100% similarity. These NUMT lengths were not significantly different from those in <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i> (Pâ=â0.07â0.72, t-test) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig4">4</a>). These results suggest that the Sphaeropleales and the Chlamydomonadales have similar amounts of ongoing mitochondrial DNA transfer to the nucleus. However, in the Sphaeropleales, <i>cox2a</i> has not been transferred to the nuclear genome; this might be caused by unusual codon usage in this order<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="RodrÃ­guez-Salinas, E. et al. Lineage-specific fragmentation and nuclear relocation of the mitochondrial cox2 gene in chlorophycean green algae (Chlorophyta). Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 64, 166â176 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2267">17</a></sup>. If the gene is transferred, it cannot be translated correctly to protein, and the DNA fragment may be eliminated immediately. Exceptionally, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> possessed many more NUMTs than the other Sphaeropleales, 339 NUMTs with 32â2,108âbp with 78â100% similarity (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S5</a>), which were significantly larger in size than those of <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i> (Pâ=â0.002, t-test) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig4">4</a>). This may be explained by the lower gene density of <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>. It had large spacer regions (3,647âbp on average) and introns (462âbp on average), therefore, it is likely that mitochondrial DNA fragments are transferred easily into the nucleus without disturbing genes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="548" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Comparison of NUMTs of <i>Raphidocelis subcapitata, Monoraphidium neglectum</i>, <i>Tetradesmus obliquus</i>, <i>Chromochloris zofingiensis</i>,Â and <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i>. Bars represent average length of NUMTs; the number of NUMTs are shown above the bars. P values (<i>t</i>-test) are shown.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Conserved gene repertories for lipid metabolism in the Sphaeropleales</h3><p>Some Sphaeropleales species are used for the production of biomass, particularly biofuels. <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> accumulate palmitate (C16:0) and oleate (C18:1) as major lipid constituents, which may be suitable feedstock for biodiesel production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2351">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Mandal, S. &amp; Mallick, N. Microalga Scenedesmus obliquus as a potential source for biodiesel production. Appl. Microbiol. Biotechnol. 84, 281â291 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2354">34</a></sup>. A congener of <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, <i>M</i>. <i>contortum</i>, can also be useful for biofuel production because of its robust growth, with efficient neutral lipid accumulation, and a favourable fatty acid profile under nitrogen starvation conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Bogen, C. et al. Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour. Technol. 133, 622â626 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2371">13</a></sup>. <i>M</i>. <i>contortum</i> and <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> mainly accumulate palmitate and oleate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Bogen, C. et al. Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour. Technol. 133, 622â626 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2388">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Nascimento, I. A. et al. Screening microalgae strains for biodiesel production: lipid productivity and estimation of fuel quality based on fatty acids profiles as selective criteria. BioEnergy Res. 6, 1â13 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2391">35</a></sup>. We searched for genes related to lipid metabolism in the Sphaeropleales, based on metabolic pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2395">10</a></sup>, and compared them to those of <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i>. They shared all the genes except for enoyl-acyl-carrier-protein reductase (EAR) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig5">5a</a>). The gene for EAR in <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> could not be detected by our homology search; however, Bogen <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2417">10</a></sup> reported the presence of EAR. The Sphaeropleales possessed more genes for acyl-CoA:diacylglycerol acyltransferase (DGAT) than <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig5">5a</a>). DGAT catalyses the last step in triacylglycerol (TAG) biosynthesis in the acyl-CoA dependent pathway, and is composed of two families with similar structure, DGAT type1 and DGAT type2 (also known as DGTT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Turchetto-Zolet, A. C. et al. Evolutionary view of acyl-CoA diacylglycerol acyltransferase (DGAT), a key enzyme in neutral lipid biosynthesis. BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 263 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2431">36</a></sup>. Roth <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Roth, M. S. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly and transcriptome of the green alga Chromochloris zofingiensis illuminates astaxanthin production. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 114, E4296âE4305 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2437">12</a></sup> discussed the difficulties for annotation of these genes because of their high sequence diversities, and 11 reported putative genes for DGAT type1 or 2. In our analyses, although the exact number of genes for DGAT with diacylglycerol acyltransferase activity could not be confidently determined, <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> tentatively possessed 11 and 12 genes for DGAT, respectively, and most of them were homologs of DGAT type2 by our phylogenetic analysis. The DGAT type2 genes of the Sphaeropleales were present in four clades (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig5">5b</a>), and each clade included other green algae, suggesting that the increase in genes originated in gene duplication. An increase in DGAT genes is found in other oleaginous organisms, such as <i>Nannochloropsis</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wang, D. et al. Nannochloropsis genomes reveal evolution of microalgal oleaginous traits. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004094 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2461">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Radakovits, R. et al. Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat. Commun. 3, 686 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2464">38</a></sup>. In these species, the increase in DGAT type2 genes originated in their endosymbiont via EGT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wang, D. et al. Nannochloropsis genomes reveal evolution of microalgal oleaginous traits. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004094 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2468">37</a></sup>, whereas the gene number increase in the Sphaeropleales was probably caused by gene duplication in their ancestor. For the other genes related to lipid biosynthesis, the Sphaeropleales possessed a large number of genes for ACC, KAR, and KAS1/2 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig5">5a</a>) as mentioned in Bogen <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2478">10</a></sup>. These findings imply that the large number of DGAT genes may be related to high lipid productivity in the Sphaeropleales.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1084" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Pathways of free fatty acids and triacylglycerol in the Sphaeropleales. (<b>a</b>) Putative pathways and gene numbers of free fatty acids and triacylglycerol in the Sphaeropleales. The metabolic pathways were based on Bogen <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2497">10</a></sup>. Enzymes are shown in bold type. Bars represent the number of genes of <i>Raphidocelis subcapitata</i> (blue), <i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i> (red), <i>Tetradesmus obliquus</i> (grey), <i>Chromochloris zofingiensis</i> (orange),Â and <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> (cyan). (<b>b</b>) Unrooted ML phylogenetic tree of DGAT2. Blue-coloured operational taxonomic units represent Sphaeropleales proteins. Green-coloured clades include the Sphaeropleales. Bootstrap support (BP) is indicated above the lines where BP is more than 50%. The ML analysis was performed using IQ-tree 1.5.5 with 215 amino acids with LGâ+âFâ+âG4 model. Non-parametric bootstrapping was performed 100 times.
