ACC: acetyl-CoA carboxylase; ACP: acyl carrier protein; MAT: acyl-carrier-protein S-malonyltranferase; KAS3: beta-ketoacyl-acyl carrier-protein synthase III; KAR: 3-oxoacyl-(acyl-carrier-protein) reductase; HD: hydrolyases; EAR: enoyl-acyl carrier protein reductase; KAS1/2: beta-ketoacyl-acyl-carrier-protein synthase I/II; PAH: palmitoyl-protein thioesterase; OAH: oleoyl-(acyl-carrier-protein) hydrolase; GK: glycerol kinase; GPAT: glycerol-3-phosphate O-acyltransferase; AGPAT: 1-acetylglycerol-3-phosphate O-acyltransferase; PP: phosphatidate phosphatase; DGAT: acyl-CoA:diacylglcerol acyltransferase; PDAT: phospholipid:diacylglycerol acyltransferase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Variation in nutrient transporter genes and environmental adaptation in the Sphaeropleales</h3><p>The Sphaeropleales are known generally as a dominant group in freshwater and adapted to different environmental conditions e.g.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Baldisserotto, C. et al. Salinity promotes growth of freshwater Neochloris oleoabundans UTEX 1185 (Sphaeropleales, Chlorophyta): morphophysiological aspects. Phycologia 51, 700â710 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2536">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Fawley, M. W., Fawley, K. P. &amp; Buchheim, M. A. Molecular diversity among communities of freshwater microchlorophytes. Microb. Ecol. 48, 489â499 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2539">40</a></sup>. Moreover, Sphaeropleales species have high sensitivity to exogenous substances<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Nagai, T., Taya, K. &amp; Yoda, I. Comparative toxicity of 20 herbicides to 5 periphytic algae and the relationship with mode of action. Environ. Toxicol. Chem. 35, 368â375 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2543">41</a></sup>. To characterize the adaptive potential of the Sphaeropleales to environmental variability, we compared transporter genes among the Sphaeropleales and <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i>. Most of the transporter gene repertoires for nutrients and metals were similar among the Sphaeropleales and <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S6</a>); however, the Sphaeropleales possessed markedly greater numbers of genes for H<sup>+</sup>/hexose transporters (2.A.1.1, TCAD ID), amino acid permeases (2.A.18.2), peptide transporters (2.A.17.3), aquaporin (1.A.8.8), and metal-nicotianamine transporters (2.A.67.2) than <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i> (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 The number of genes for several transporters.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The H<sup>+</sup>/hexose cotransporter is functionally related to intake of glucose from the outside of cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Williams, L. E., Lemoine, R. &amp; Sauer, N. Sugar transporters in higher plants â a diversity of roles and complex regulation. Trends Plant Sci. 5, 283â290 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2882">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ozcan, S. &amp; Johnston, M. Function and regulation of yeast hexose transporters. Microbiol. Mol. Biol. Rev. 63, 554â69 (1999)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2885">43</a></sup>. A trebouxiophyte, <i>Parachlorella kessleri</i>, also has H<sup>+</sup>/hexose cotransporter genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Sauer, N. &amp; Tanner, W. The hexose carrier from Chlorella. FEBS Lett. 259, 43â46 (1989)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2894">44</a></sup>. They are categorized in three classes in green algae, based on the phylogenetic relationships<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Gao, C. et al. Oil accumulation mechanisms of the oleaginous microalga Chlorella protothecoides revealed through its genome, transcriptomes, and proteomes. BMC Genomics 15, 582 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2899">45</a></sup>. In the classes, genes in the HUP-like class are increased uniquely in mixotrophic green algae (e.g. <i>Chlorella</i> and <i>Auxenochlorella</i>), and thus the <i>HUP-like</i> is possibly related to the heterotrophic lifestyle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Gao, C. et al. Oil accumulation mechanisms of the oleaginous microalga Chlorella protothecoides revealed through its genome, transcriptomes, and proteomes. BMC Genomics 15, 582 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2912">45</a></sup>. Expression of this gene is induced during heterotrophic growth in <i>P</i>. <i>kessleri</i>. The mRNA was not detected in photosynthetically-grown cells, but immediately induced by glucose under darkness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Hilgarth, C., Sauer, N. &amp; Tanner, W. Glucose increases the expression of the ATP/ADP translocator and the glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate dehydrogenase genes in Chlorella. J. Biol. Chem. 266, 24044â24047 (1991)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2923">46</a></sup>. Interestingly, our phylogenetic analysis showed that <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> had 6, 3, 4, and 7 <i>HUP-like</i> genes, respectively, and they were monophyletic with heterotrophic or mixotrophic trebouxiophytes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig6">6a</a>). This result suggests that the <i>HUP-like</i> genes of the trebouxiophytes and the Sphaeropleales had the same origin, and the genes were acquired in the common ancestor of the trebouxiophytes and the Sphaeropleales but were lacking in the Chlamydomonadales. However, it is also possible, although unlikely, that the gene has been acquired independently in the trebouxiophytes and the Sphaeropleales via horizontal gene transfer and increase in each group. Genome information of the OCC clade is needed to clearly reveal their evolutionary history. Heterotrophic growth using hexose or other simple sugars (e.g. glucose) was observed in <i>Monoraphidium</i> sp.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Yu, X. et al. Isolation of a novel strain of Monoraphidium sp. and characterization of its potential application as biodiesel feedstock. Bioresour. Technol. 121, 256â262 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2965">47</a></sup>, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Camacho Rubio, F., MartÃ­nez Sancho, M. E., SÃ¡nchez Villasclaras, S. &amp; Delgado PÃ©rez, A. Influence of pH on the kinetic and yield parameters of Scenedesmus obliquus heterotrophic growth. Process Biochem 40, 133â136 (1989)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2974">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Abeliovich, A. &amp; Weisman, D. Role of heterotrophic nutrition in growth of the alga Scenedesmus obliquus in high-rate oxidation ponds. Appl. Environ. Microbiol. 35, 32â37 (1978)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2977">49</a></sup>, and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Sun, N., Wang, Y., Li, Y.-T., Huang, J.-C. &amp; Chen, F. Sugar-based growth, astaxanthin accumulation and carotenogenic transcription of heterotrophic Chlorella zofingiensis (Chlorophyta). Process Biochem. 43, 1288â1292 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2987">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Liu, J. et al. Differential lipid and fatty acid profiles of photoautotrophic and heterotrophic Chlorella zofingiensis: Assessment of algal oils for biodiesel production. Bioresour. Technol. 102, 106â110 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d61147e2990">51</a></sup>. To investigate the heterotrophic and mixotrophic growth ability of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, we cultured it with and without 0.5% glucose under light or continuous dark conditions for 9 days (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Fig6">6b</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S9</a>). Final cell concentration was the highest in the culture treatment with glucose under light; cell concentration was ~20-fold higher in this treatment than in the treatment without glucose under light. The treatment with glucose under continuous darkness had a ~6-fold higher cell concentration than the treatment without glucose under light; cells could not grow without glucose under continuous darkness. These results suggest that <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> is mixotrophic and can grow under heterotrophic conditions without photosynthesis. The cells cultured with glucose contained many lipid bodies (Supplementary FigsÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#MOESM1">S10</a>), suggesting that <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> stores excess glucose as TAG in these lipid bodies. In the case of nitrogen resources, we identified a large number of amino acid/peptide transporter genes in the Sphaeropleales, although the number of nitrate/nitrite transporter genes was not different among the Sphaeropleales and <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i>. In <i>Chlorella vulgaris</i>, glucose or glucose analogues induce the intake of amino acids through amino acid permeases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Cho, B. H., Sauer, N., Komor, E. &amp; Tanner, W. Glucose induces two amino acid transport systems in Chlorella. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 78, 3591â3594 (1981)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3035">52</a></sup>, even if inorganic nitrogen (i.e. ammonium or nitrate) is present in high amounts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Sauer, N., Komor, E. &amp; Tanner, W. Regulation and characterization of two inducible amino-acid transport systems in Chlorella vulgaris. Planta 159, 404â410 (1983)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3039">53</a></sup>. Based on these findings, it appears reasonable that the Sphaeropleales possess a large number of H<sup>+</sup>/hexose cotransporters and amino acid permeases. These transporters may contribute to their rapid growth under different nutrient conditions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-26331-6/MediaObjects/41598_2018_26331_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="990" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Phylogenetic analysis of H<sup>+</sup>/Hexose cotransporters and growth curves of <i>Raphidocelis subcapitata</i>. (<b>a</b>) ML tree of H<sup>+</sup>/hexose cotransporters in green algae. Inositol transporters are used as an outgroup. Orange-coloured operational taxonomic units represent proteins of the Sphaeropleales. Bootstrap support (BP) is indicated above the lines where BP is more than 50%. Bold lines represent 100% BP. The ML analysis was performed using IQ-tree 1.5.5 with 348 amino acids with LGâ+âFâ+âG4 model. Non-parametric bootstrapping was performed 100 times. (<b>b</b>) Growth curves of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> under autotrophic and heterotrophic conditions. Blue, red, grey, and orange lines represent cultures under light without glucose, continuous dark without glucose, light with glucose, and continuous dark with glucose, respectively. The three independent cultures for each cultivated condition were counted three times. Error bars represent 95% confidence intervals.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i> and <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i> can grow under highly salinity conditions (up to 1% NaCl)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3098">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Gan, X., Shen, G., Xin, B. &amp; Li, M. Simultaneous biological desalination and lipid production by Scenedesmus obliquus cultured with brackish water. Desalination 400, 1â6 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3101">54</a></sup>. The adaptation to high salt stress may be conferred by the large number of genes for aquaporin (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#Tab2">2</a>). Aquaporin can passively transport small polar molecules, such as water, across cell membranes in different species of algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Anderberg, H. I., Danielson, J. Ã. &amp; Johanson, U. Algal MIPs, high diversity and conserved motifs. BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 110 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3109">55</a></sup>, and is likely to be used for controlling intracellular osmotic pressure. Multiple metal-nicotianamine transporters may be related to high sensitivity of the Sphaeropleales to exogenous metals. Metal-nicotianamine transporters transport various metals, such as Fe(III), Fe(II), Ni(II), Zn(II), Cu(II), Mn(II), and Cd(II) as metal-PS (phytosiderophores) or metal-NA (nicotianamine) chelates in <i>Zea mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Schaaf, G. et al. ZmYS1 functions as a proton-coupled symporter for phytosiderophore- and nicotianamine-chelated metals. J. Biol. Chem. 279, 9091â9096 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3115">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Murata, Y. et al. A specific transporter for iron(III)-phytosiderophore in barley roots. Plant J. 46, 563â572 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3118">57</a></sup>. In <i>Scenedesmus</i>, organic chelators are released in inorganic medium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Benderliev, K. M. &amp; Ivanova, N. I. Determination of available iron in mixtures of organic chelators secreted by Scenedesmus incrassatulus. Biotechnol. Tech. 10, 513â518 (1996)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3125">58</a></sup>, and uptake of iron is regulated by siderophore secretion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Benderliev, K. M. &amp; Ivanova, N. I. High-affinity siderophore-mediated iron-transport system in the green alga Scenedesmus incrassatulus. Planta 193, 163â166 (1994)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3129">59</a></sup>. These findings suggest that the large number of genes for metal-nicotianamine transporters in <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> may induce high sensitivity to different metals. <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> also possessed multiple copies of genes for metal-nicotianamine transporters, suggesting that the Sphaeropleales might have high sensitivity to metals. In our homology search, there were few genes for metal-nicotianamine transporters among green algae, except for the Sphaeropleales; however, their origins are unknown because of their sequence diversity. The increase in transporters in the Sphaeropleales is likely to be related to their lifestyle. In the Sphaeropleales cell cycle, the stage with motility (e.g. flagellates) is not dominant or not known<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yamagishi, T., Yamaguchi, H., Suzuki, S., Horie, Y. &amp; Tatarazako, N. Cell reproductive patterns in the green alga Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata (=Selenastrum capricornutum) and their variations under exposure to the typical toxicants potassium dichromate and 3,5-DCP. PLoS One 12, e0171259 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3159">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Trainor, F. P. &amp; Burg, C. A. Scenedesmus obliquus sexuality. Science 148, 1094â1095 (1965)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3162">61</a></sup>. Immobility may force cells to adapt to different environmental conditions aided by their numerous transporters.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>The Sphaeropleales are a dominant group of green algae and contain species important in ecosystems and those with potential for applied usage. In this study, we sequenced the nuclear, plastid, and mitochondrial genomes of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, and performed comparative analyses of Sphaeropleales species. The plastid and mitochondrial genomes provided insights into the phylogenetic relationships of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and the complex evolutionary histories in the Sphaeropleales. The nuclear genome of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was the more compact genome (i.e. assembly size) than those of <i>M</i>. <i>neglectum</i>, <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i>. The gene repertoire was conserved in Sphaeropleales species. Comparison of transporter genes indicated that the Sphaeropleales have the potential to adapt to different natural environmental conditions. These findings have implications for future ecological research and applications such as biomarkers and screening of highly oleaginous algae.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Culture</h3><p><i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> NIES-35 (=ATCC22662) is a strain widely used in environmental bioassays e.g.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yamagishi, T., Yamaguchi, H., Suzuki, S., Horie, Y. &amp; Tatarazako, N. Cell reproductive patterns in the green alga Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata (=Selenastrum capricornutum) and their variations under exposure to the typical toxicants potassium dichromate and 3,5-DCP. PLoS One 12, e0171259 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3230">60</a></sup>. It is an axenic strain that is available from the Microbial Culture Collection at the National Institute for Environmental Studies, Japan (<a href="http://mcc.nies.go.jp">http://mcc.nies.go.jp</a>). The strain was cultivated at 20âÂ°C in C medium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Ichimura, T. Sexual cell division and conjugation-papilla formation in sexual reproduction of Closterium strigosum. In International Symposium on Seaweed Research, 7th, Sapporo 208â214 (University of Tokyo Press, 1971)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3241">62</a></sup> under white LED (~20 Âµmol photons/m<sup>2</sup>/s) with 12âh:12âh light:dark cycles in 300âmL glass flasks.</p><p>For tests of cultivation under mixotrophic or heterotrophic conditions, the cells were cultivated in 6âmL of C medium or C medium with 0.5% glucose in glass test tubes under the above light conditions or continuous darkness. Three separate <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> cultures were grown in each type of medium and light conditions. The cells were shaken using a TAITEC small size shaker NR-3 (TAITEC, Tokyo, Japan) at ~150 rotations per minute. The initial cell concentration was approximately 1.7âÃâ10<sup>5</sup> cells/mL. Cell concentrations were counted three times using a haemocytometer.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">DNA and RNA extraction</h3><p>For DNA extraction, <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> was cultured for 2 weeks in 500âmL of C medium. Cells were collected by gentle centrifugation and ground in a pre-cooled mortar with liquid nitrogen and 50âmg of 0.1âmm glass beads (Bertin, Rockville, MD, USA). The cells were incubated with 600âÂµL of CTAB extraction buffer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Lang, B. F. &amp; Burger, G. Purification of mitochondrial and plastid DNA. Nat. Protoc. 2, 652â660 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3272">63</a></sup> at 65âÂ°C for 1âh. DNA was separated by mixing with 500âÂµL of chloroform and centrifuging at 20,000âÃâ<i>g</i> for 1âmin; it was concentrated by standard EtOH precipitation. For RNA sequencing, cells were cultured for a week in 100âmLâC medium. To acquire whole expressed genes, RNA was extracted twice just before light- and dark-phase. Cells were collected and ground as described above, and RNA was extracted using the RNeasy Mini Kit (Qiagen, Hilden, Germany). DNA contamination was removed using the TURBO DNA-free Kit (Thermo Fisher Scientific, Waltham, MA, USA). Equal amounts of light- and dark-RNA samples were mixed and sequenced at the same time.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Sequencing</h3><p>For DNA libraries, we prepared paired-end (~550âbp insert) and mate-pair (~3â4 kbp insert) libraries. The paired-end library was prepared using the TruSeq Nano DNA Library Prep Kit for NeoPrep (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA) with the NeoPrep system (Illumina) following the manufacturerâs protocol. The mate-pair library was prepared using the Nextera Mate Pair Sample Preparation Kit (Illumina) following the manufacturerâs protocol. We also prepared a paired-end RNA library (~550âbp insert) using the NEBNext Ultra Directional RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (New England BioLabs, Inc., Ipswich, MA, USA). mRNA was purified using the NEBNext Poly(A) mRNA Magnetic Isolation Module (New England BioLabs). All libraries were sequenced on the MiSeq sequencing system (Illumina) using the MiSeq Reagent Kit v3, 600 cycles (300âbpâÃâ2), and the MiSeq Reagent Kit v2, 150 cycles (75âbpâÃâ2), for the paired-end and mate-pair libraries, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Assembly and annotation</h3><p>We acquired 3,847,746, 16,768,250, and 1,394,371 read pairs for DNA paired-end, mate-pair, and RNA paired-end libraries, respectively. The reads were deposited in DDBJ/Genbank/ENA with accession numbers DRR090198 (DNA paired-end), DRR090199 (DNA mate-pair), and DRR090200 (RNA paired-end). The sequences were trimmed using Trimmomatic 0.36<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3295">64</a></sup> with default options. The DNA paired-end reads were used for <i>in silico</i> genome size estimation by Jellyfish 2.2.6<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="MarÃ§ais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â70 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3302">65</a></sup> with the 17-mer option. The genome was assembled using SPAdes Genome Assembler 3.9.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Bankevich, A. et al. SPAdes: a new genome assembly algorithm and its applications to single-cell sequencing. J. Comput. Biol. 19, 455â477 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3306">66</a></sup> with default options. Scaffolding was performed using SSPACE-standard 3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578â579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3310">67</a></sup>, and some gaps were closed by GapFiller v1.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Boetzer, M. &amp; Pirovano, W. Toward almost closed genomes with GapFiller. Genome Biol. 13, R56 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3315">68</a></sup>. To extract plastid and mitochondrial sequences, we performed a blastx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389â3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3319">69</a></sup> search using available plastid and mitochondrial proteins of the Sphaeropleales as a query. The plastid and mitochondrial genomes were automatically annotated using Prokka v1.11<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Seemann, T. Prokka: rapid prokaryotic genome annotation. Bioinformatics 30, 2068â2069 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3323">70</a></sup>, and manually curated on an Artemis genome browser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Rutherford, K. et al. Artemis: sequence visualization and annotation. Bioinformatics 16, 944â945 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3327">71</a></sup>. rRNA and tRNA were also searched using RNAmmer 1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Lagesen, K. et al. RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 3100â3108 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3331">72</a></sup> and tRNAscan-SE 2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Schattner, P., Brooks, A. N. &amp; Lowe, T. M. The tRNAscan-SE, snoscan and snoGPS web servers for the detection of tRNAs and snoRNAs. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W686â689 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3335">73</a></sup>, respectively. Group I and II introns were initially detected using RNAweasel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Lang, B. F., Laforest, M.-J. &amp; Burger, G. Mitochondrial introns: a critical view. Trends Genet. 23, 119â125 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3340">74</a></sup>, and curated by alignments with homologs. Tandem repeats were found using Tandem Repeats Finder 4.09<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3344">75</a></sup>. Gene models of the nuclear scaffolds were constructed following the funannotate pipeline 0.3.7 (<a href="https://github.com/nextgenusfs/funannotate">https://github.com/nextgenusfs/funannotate</a>). To acquire evidence for gene model construction, we mapped RNA-seq reads to the scaffolds using HISAT2 2.0.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357â360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3355">76</a></sup>, and constructed transcript-based gene models using Trinity 2.2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494â1512 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3359">77</a></sup> and PASA pipeline 2.0.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654â5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3363">78</a></sup>. For the nuclear genome of <i>T</i>. <i>obliquus</i>, because annotation was not available, we annotated the scaffolds (accession number GCA_900108755.1). Repeat regions of the scaffolds were soft-masked using RepeatModeler and RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>). Gene models were predicted using AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Stanke, M., SchÃ¶ffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3381">79</a></sup>, for which training was performed using BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3385">28</a></sup> with the eukaryote dataset. The nuclear, plastid, and mitochondrial genomes of <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> were deposited in DDBJ/Genbank/ENA with accession numbers BDRX01000001âBDRX01000300, AP018038, and AP018037, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Phylogenetic analyses</h3><p>To infer the phylogenetic relationships between <i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i> and other species of the Sphaeropleales, we performed phylogenetic analyses using plastid and mitochondrial genome-encoded proteins. For plastid proteins, we used 55 proteins in the dataset described by FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡ <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="FuÄÃ­kovÃ¡, K., Lewis, P. O. &amp; Lewis, L. A. Chloroplast phylogenomic data from the green algal order Sphaeropleales (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta) reveal complex patterns of sequence evolution. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 98, 176â183 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3413">20</a></sup> (AtpA, AptB, AtpE, AtpF, AtpH, AtpI, CcsA, CemA, ClpP, PetB, PetD, PetG, PetL, PsaA, PsaB, PsaC, PsaJ, PsbA, PsbB, PsbC, PsbD, PsbE, PsbF, PsbI, PsbJ, PsbK, PsbL, PsbM, PsbN, PsbT, PsbZ, RbcL, Rpl2, Rpl5, Rpl14, Rpl16, Rpl20, Rpl23, Rpl36, RpoA, RpoBa, RpoBb, RpoC2, Rps3, Rps4, Rps7, Rps8, Rps9, Rps11, Rps12, Rps14, Rps18, Rps19, TufA, and Ycf3). The dataset was composed of 12 organisms in the Sphaeropleales. The complete plastid genome of <i>Ourococcus multisporus</i> was unavailable, but instead, we used its protein sequences. <i>Mychonastes homosphaera</i> was excluded from the dataset because of its rapid evolutionary rate. <i>D</i>. <i>salina</i> and <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i> were used as an outgroup. For the mitochondrial dataset, we used all mitochondrial genome encoding proteins: Atp6, Atp9, Cob, Cox1, Cox2a, Cox3, Nad1, Nad2, Nad3, Nad4, Nad4L, Nad5, and Nad6. The dataset was composed of 17 organisms. <i>Stigeoclonium helveticum</i> (Chaetophorales) was used as an outgroup. Both sequences were aligned using MAFFT v7.221<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Toh, H. Recent developments in the MAFFT multiple sequence alignment program. Brief. Bioinform. 9, 286â98 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3439">80</a></sup>, and highly divergent regions were manually trimmed with MEGA 6.0.6<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725â2729 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3444">81</a></sup>. Substitution models were tested using IQ-TREE 1.4.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Nguyen, L.-T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268â274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3448">82</a></sup>. Maximum likelihood (ML) analyses were performed using RAxML 8.2.9<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3452">83</a></sup> with the LGâ+âGAMMAâ+âFâ+âI model. Non-parametric bootstrap analyses were replicated 200 times. Bayesian analyses were performed using MrBayes v3.2.6<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Ronquist, F. et al. Mrbayes 3.2: Efficient bayesian phylogenetic inference and model choice across a large model space. Syst. Biol. 61, 539â542 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3456">84</a></sup> with the same substitution model. The inference consisted of 1,000,000 generations with sampling every 1,000 generations using four Metropolis-coupled Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMCMC) simulations. Two separate runs were performed, and Bayesian posterior probabilities were calculated from the majority rule consensus of the tree sampled after the initial 250 burn-in trees.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Identification of gene families, NUMTs, and genes for lipid biosynthesis and transporters</h3><p>Gene families were searched using TreeFam 9<sup>29</sup> with an e-value cut-off of &lt;1E<sup>â5</sup>. NUMTs were searched by blastn with the mitochondrial genomes as a query, with an e-value cut-off of &lt;1E<sup>â3</sup>. Genes for lipid biosynthesis were based on Bogen <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3476">10</a></sup>, and searched using blastp with the proteins of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> as a query, with an e-value cut-off of &lt;1E<sup>â10</sup>. Transporters were identified using blastp with the Transporter Classification Database (TCDB)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Saier, M. H. et al. The Transporter Classification Database (TCDB): recent advances. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D372âD379 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-26331-6#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d61147e3486">85</a></sup> as a query, with an e-value cut-off of &lt;1E<sup>â5</sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Data availability</h3><p>The strain used in this study (<i>R</i>. <i>subcapitata</i>, NIES-35) is available from the Microbial Culture Collection at the National Institute for Environmental Studies (NIES) (<a href="http://mcc.nies.go.jp">http://mcc.nies.go.jp</a>), Japan. The DNA paired-end, mate-pair, and RNA paired-end reads were deposited in DDBJ/Genbank/ENA with accession numbers DRR090198, DRR090199, and DRR090200, respectively. The nuclear, plastid, and mitochondrial genomes were deposited in DDBJ/Genbank/ENA with accession numbers BDRX01000001âBDRX01000300, AP018038, and AP018037, respectively.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="Bib1" data-title="References"><div class="c-article-section" id="Bib1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Bib1">References</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Bib1-content"><div data-container-section="references"><ol class="c-article-references"><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="F.
